    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2562287478" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2562287478" />
    <a title="Lucas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53077063-lucas"><img alt="Lucas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555813341p2/53077063.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2562287478?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lucas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lucas" href="/user/show/53077063-lucas">Lucas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140446814" href="/book/show/6367153-the-mahabharata">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=ancient-literature">ancient-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=sanskrit">sanskrit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=epic-poetry">epic-poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=unknown-author">unknown-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2562287478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6123549318257370311">I've done it. I have finished the Mahabharata. I feel like I've climbed up the Himalaya and back.<br/><br/>So where do I begin? You've heard about massive works in the history of literature, but this one dwarves them all. Its scope is impossibly big. With 200,000 verses it is the longest poem ever written; even in the abridged prose version that I have read, the superb translation by John D. Smith, it is one-thousand pages long. I genuinely believe that a man could spend a lifetime lost inside the Bharata</span>
  <span id="freeText6123549318257370311" style="display:none">I've done it. I have finished the Mahabharata. I feel like I've climbed up the Himalaya and back.<br /><br />So where do I begin? You've heard about massive works in the history of literature, but this one dwarves them all. Its scope is impossibly big. With 200,000 verses it is the longest poem ever written; even in the abridged prose version that I have read, the superb translation by John D. Smith, it is one-thousand pages long. I genuinely believe that a man could spend a lifetime lost inside the Bharata. It is gargantuan. For long passages it feels repetitive and frustrating; then you get to the trascendent moments and oh my it is so good.<br /><br />"That which is not found here is to be found nowhere", reads one of its most famous lines and I suspect it to be true. At its core, The Mahabharata is an epic poem along the lines of the Iliad, a sprawling war narrative with over-the-top violence and bigger than life archetypical heroes. Had I been born in India or around, I get the feeling I would know of Arjuna, Yudishtira, Bishma, as I know of Odysseus, Hector and Aquilles. But then the book is just so much more. In the multi-layered plot you get numberous creation-myths, fantastic stories within stories, romances, countless fables and two huge religious treaties that would put the Bible to shame. It is seriously that impressive. Indian literature is so cool.<br /><br />Note aside for the great storytelling: the narrative device is an example of classic orality, yet it feels so modern. The Mahabharata is structured like multiple stories within other stories in different times, with long digressions that lead to more stories and in a cool meta-game the Mahabharata itself is inside the narrative, as a poem written by one of the hundreds of characters that populate this rich world of its own.<br /><br />This is an awe-inspiring literary jewel, a total work of which I have just seen but a glimpse of its total beauty. It takes a particular state of mind to decide to go ahead and read this mammoth: you have to love literature above all things and you have to be a bit of a headcase take up this book. But if you fulfil those requirements, read the Mahabharata. You won't be dissapointed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6123549318257370311" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer329076982038521095">Anyone wondering why I moved so many books to my "on hold" shelf, this is why!<br/><br/>I'm reading this in preparation to read Shashi Tharoor's "The Great Indian Novel," which is supposedly based on this in some way. Tharoor's book is about the Indian fight for independence from Britain....so I'm thinking he uses the metaphor of the warring cousins as the stage-setter. <br/><br/>The Maha is a B-I-G book for sure. But as always, Penguin delivers. They are my go-to publishers for translations. This edition is abridg</span>
  <span id="freeText329076982038521095" style="display:none">Anyone wondering why I moved so many books to my "on hold" shelf, this is why!<br /><br />I'm reading this in preparation to read Shashi Tharoor's "The Great Indian Novel," which is supposedly based on this in some way. Tharoor's book is about the Indian fight for independence from Britain....so I'm thinking he uses the metaphor of the warring cousins as the stage-setter. <br /><br />The Maha is a B-I-G book for sure. But as always, Penguin delivers. They are my go-to publishers for translations. This edition is abridged, but rather than simply omitting sections, Smith summarizes them -- so you still get the gist. He just isn't boring you with all the details. A highly commendable job!<br /><br />*****************************<br /><br />FINISHED!!!!!!!!!!!!<br /><br />I have never spent so much time on a single book. But it was worth it. Yeah, there are long sections of rather dull laundry-list details of the war or whatever.....but after a while you learn how to either skim them or accept it. <br /><br />It was difficult to be so interested in the main story, only to see it divert off into parables for long periods of time. But, these parables tell a great deal about Hinduism. Bhisma's death monologue is too long, but the sections dealing with death/rebirth and such things were really good (I skipped the dharma of giving cows). <br /><br />In the end, I'm glad I read it. One, because now I understand Tharoor's book on a whole different level that would be lost if I hadn't (handy Wiki character table aside). Two, because I feel a great sense of accomplishment. And three, because not many other books could give me such insight into things that make India what it is.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1667206719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7686691588397108525">Before starting this review, I want to say that I absolutely love this epic story of Mahabharata. I have followed the TV Shows and short stories quite passionately. <br/><br/>It's an epic saga and having a fair amount of knowledge about the tidings of this story, I wanted to read it too. I asked a couple of my friends and researched online to find the best book in English and I stumbled upon this one. <br/>Mr. Rajagopalachari has written this book just for the sake of it. It is as if he has done a great favor </span>
  <span id="freeText7686691588397108525" style="display:none">Before starting this review, I want to say that I absolutely love this epic story of Mahabharata. I have followed the TV Shows and short stories quite passionately. <br /><br />It's an epic saga and having a fair amount of knowledge about the tidings of this story, I wanted to read it too. I asked a couple of my friends and researched online to find the best book in English and I stumbled upon this one. <br />Mr. Rajagopalachari has written this book just for the sake of it. It is as if he has done a great favor on humanity by writing this one. He has just translated the old scripture in English with bare minimum effort. He does not know how to write. He has just told the story in the most un interesting and monotonous manner. He has not gone into characters. He has not given any reasoning behind any twist and turn. He has omitted many facts, many sub plots which would have helped in developing the story overall. <br /><br />He has not written anything about Shakuni. About his rage when his beloved sister Gandhari was married off to a blind man. The author fails to tell us the story behind sharing of Draupadi between five brothers. <br /><br />Also, he fails to give a chronological account of events. He kills the suspense of the battle by giving many spoilers way before the battle actually started.<br /><br />He just wanted to write this or probably force this epic into a 400-500 page novel. <br />It would have been wonderful if he could show us the story happening instead of doing just a moral duty of telling it.<br /><br />I am hugely disappointed with this book!!<br />In case anyone can suggest me a better book on Mahabharata, please do. I would love to read a good book on this epic.</span>

    <a title="Anubhuti Sharma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21090161-anubhuti-sharma"><img alt="Anubhuti Sharma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420460718p2/21090161.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1238495601?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anubhuti Sharma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anubhuti Sharma" href="/user/show/21090161-anubhuti-sharma">Anubhuti Sharma</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1238495601" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15107855653499000415">For beginners and enthusiasts of the Mahabharata, this book would undoubtedly prove to be a well justified and a rational choice. This book is truly a masterpiece by the grand old scholar C. Rajagopalachari and a testimony to his genius is the continued circulation of this book for more than half a century since its was first published.<br/> <br/>Through simple language and undiluted translation Rajaji builds the epic saga in a manner that is enjoyable and easy to recollect. Its uninterrupted chronologica</span>
  <span id="freeText15107855653499000415" style="display:none">For beginners and enthusiasts of the Mahabharata, this book would undoubtedly prove to be a well justified and a rational choice. This book is truly a masterpiece by the grand old scholar C. Rajagopalachari and a testimony to his genius is the continued circulation of this book for more than half a century since its was first published.<br /> <br />Through simple language and undiluted translation Rajaji builds the epic saga in a manner that is enjoyable and easy to recollect. Its uninterrupted chronological narration - that begins from the Adiparva, proceeds unblocked through the Great War and ends rightly at the Swargarohanparva - provide a wholesome experience to the reader that few books on the Mahabharata have. Unlike many prevalent versions, this book does not omit the colourful mythological stories that embellish this saga and leaves them up to the young scholars to analyse and evaluate. However, though it records the numerous episodes of this epic briefly in about a hundred stories, its description misses out the warmth and richness of passionate storytellers. On several occasions, one wishes it to be more interactive, than dry, and interpretative, than plane, in its writing style. Nonetheless, the remarkable preciseness of this story collection that wraps the world's longest poem within a 500-paged single volume, strikes among its best features. Priced humbly at Rs. 190, the Mahabharata by C. Rajagopalachari is the perfect handbook for all those beginners who just want to know what the epic is all about.</span>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12372366948434333349">Mahabharata. The greatest story ever told. Discount that this is a rendition by C. Rajagopalachari, you can bank upon his virtuous intellect and assiduous efforts to bring to you the greatest epic in the world veritably unabridged and undefiled. <br/><br/>I am no one to review the Mahabharata and any efforts by me to rate or judge this grandiose epic would do nothing to attenuate the indelible glory of this literary creation.<br/><br/>If there is a book that projects all imaginable and thinkable facets of human exp</span>
  <span id="freeText12372366948434333349" style="display:none">Mahabharata. The greatest story ever told. Discount that this is a rendition by C. Rajagopalachari, you can bank upon his virtuous intellect and assiduous efforts to bring to you the greatest epic in the world veritably unabridged and undefiled. <br /><br />I am no one to review the Mahabharata and any efforts by me to rate or judge this grandiose epic would do nothing to attenuate the indelible glory of this literary creation.<br /><br />If there is a book that projects all imaginable and thinkable facets of human expression and emotion so astutely and lucidly then it is this very one. The Mahabharata deftly incorporates all aspects of human mindset and presents it before us that you don't read it, you literally live it. It's not a book whose pages you turn engrossed inadvertently, you experience life several times over. Almost everywhere will you find instances you can relate to and almost say out aloud "How true". The amazing genius of this epic will teach you all you wish to know about mankind, ethics, values and above all, it's central themes of the immutable law of nature : The Constancy of Change, the Congress of Universal "Dharma" and "Swadharma".<br /><br />Read it to be enlightened by several years. Definitely my personal favourite. Try and read the Sanskrit version of the Bhagwad Gita with English translation by Swami Prabhupada too. </span>

<div id="review_266542847" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/266542847" />
    <a title="Maria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4705597-maria"><img alt="Maria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348555210p2/4705597.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/266542847?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria" href="/user/show/4705597-maria">Maria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4705597-maria?shelf=in-2012">in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4705597-maria?shelf=adulthood">adulthood</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer266542847" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6399110857684587722">The Mahabharata is an Ancient Sanskrit epic, believed to have been begun in the 8th or 9th century BC, and 'completed' in the 4th century BC.<br/><br/>The whole thing is about ten times the size of the Iliad or the Odyssey, but I read an abridged version— the parts that contain the actual story of the epic, and less of the moralizing sections that explained to ancient Hindus how they should behave.<br/><br/>The epic follows the five Pandu/Pandav brothers (most concerned with Yadhisthir, the eldest, and Arjun, the m</span>
  <span id="freeText6399110857684587722" style="display:none">The Mahabharata is an Ancient Sanskrit epic, believed to have been begun in the 8th or 9th century BC, and 'completed' in the 4th century BC.<br /><br />The whole thing is about ten times the size of the Iliad or the Odyssey, but I read an abridged version— the parts that contain the actual story of the epic, and less of the moralizing sections that explained to ancient Hindus how they should behave.<br /><br />The epic follows the five Pandu/Pandav brothers (most concerned with Yadhisthir, the eldest, and Arjun, the middle child), and their war with their cousin, Duryhodan. I would, however, term Arjun the main character, or the hero— he is the one that fights Karna, his half-brother. He is the one whose sufferings and winnings are shown the most— the one that wins Draupadi, Yadhisthir's wife, and who loses his son on the battlefield.<br /><br />There's a war going on a "red-soaked plain," so I'd rate the 'roughness' in this book an 8 or a nine. I probably wouldn't have survived a day, as I wouldn't be able to draw a bow like Arjun or Karna, and I wouldn't have been able to fight against people whose births were inspired by the Sun or other Hindu gods.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6399110857684587722" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('tz6jCT9ZhxZMWceGh/IQ+jqhAcR8IYhWU0Z9vph7y+1e/47MZTl7r+ykR2ek6X0lSZEkUACg1dZt8xkndkFa0w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('nKusYm4K/1itPedLqpCGeLeO9Rr3wx3PNGnIW3wDi3J1aoGnNGoD4Q3AZ6qJi+unxL7QjotCQE8K3KzCkjkaTA==')})
  }
//]]>

Se cree que sus orígenes se remontan al siglo IV a.C. ya que forma parte de los smíriti hindúes (textos no aportados directamente por Dios, sino transmitidos por la tradición). El Ramayana es una de las obras literarias más importantes de la India, este libro estableció algunas pautas fundamentales dentro de la poesía sánscrita y ha tenido una gran influencia en el arte y la cultura del sureste de Asia, El texto de esta obra es alegórico definitivamente, cuenta la historia del semidiós Rama, quien representa todo lo bueno, y su encantada lucha contra los asuras, que ostentan el poder del mal. En la mitología hindú, asura es el símbolo equivalente al titán o demonio gigante, así que en esta epopeya tenemos el mito de la lucha entre semidioses y demonios, es decir, el combate entre los poderes de la luz y las fuerzas malignas de la oscuridad.\n\nRama constituye el ideal del hombre sabio, fuerte, justo y bueno, en él se funden todas las cualidades del gobernante y del guerrero. Everyone of whatever age, outlook, education or station in life knows the essential part of the epic and adores the main figures in it - Rama and Sita. Every child is told the story at bedtime . . . The Ramayana pervades our cultural life.\' Although the Sanskrit original was composed by Valmiki, probably around the fourth century BC, poets have produced countless variant versions in different languages. Here, drawing his inspiration from the work of an eleventh-century Tamil poet called Kamban, Narayan has used the talents of a master novelist to recreate the excitement and joy he has found in the original. It can be enjoyed and appreciated, he suggests, for its psychological insight, its spiritual depth and its practical wisdom - or just as a thrilling tale of abduction, battle and courtship played out Also recorded here are his meetings with Mahatma Gandhi, Rabindranath Tagore, Luther Burbank, the Catholic stigmatist Therese Neumann, and other celebrated spiritual personalities of East and West. \n\nAutobiography of a Yogi is at once a beautifully written account of an exceptional life and a profound introduction to the ancient science of Yoga and its time-honored tradition of meditation. The author clearly explains the subtle but definite laws behind both the ordinary events of everyday life and the extraordinary events commonly termed miracles. His absorbing life story thus becomes the background for a penetrating and unforgettable look at the ultimate mysteries of human existence. \n\nConsidered a modern spiritual classic, the book has been translated into more than twenty languages and is widely used as a text and reference work in colleges and universities. Still an integral part of India\'s cultural and religious expression, the Ramayana was originally composed by the Sanskrit poet Valmiki around 300 b.c. The epic of Prince Rama\'s betrayal, exile, and struggle to rescue his faithful wife, Sita, from the clutches of a demon and to reclaim his throne has profoundly affected the literature, art, and culture of South and Southeast Asia-an influence most likely unparalleled in the history of world literature, except, possibly, for the Bible. Throughout the centuries, countless versions of the epic have been produced in numerous formats and languages. But previous English versions have been either too short to capture the magnitude of the original; too secular in presenting what is, in effect, scripture; or dry, line-by-line translations. Now novelist Ramesh Menon has rendered the tale in lyrical prose that conveys all the beauty and excitement of the original, while making this spiritual and literary classic accessible to a new This seems a deliberate souring of an uplifting narrative. Rams refusal to remarry to produce a royal heir adds to the complexity. The intention seems to be to provoke thought on notions of fidelity, property and self-image.\n\nAnd so the mythologist and illustrator Devdutt Pattanaik retells the Ramayana, drawing attention to the many oral, visual and written retellings composed in different times, in different places, by different poets, each one trying to solve the puzzle in its own unique way. \n\nThis book approaches Ram the prime of her youth; her interactions with the women of Lanka, recipes she exchanged, emotions they shared; her connection with the earth, her mother, and with the trees, her sisters; her role as Yoga is from the Sanskrit root meaning “union,” and a sutra is a thread or aphoristic verse. The basic questions “Who am I?” “Where am I going?” “What is the purpose of life?” are asked by each new generation, and Patanjali’s answers form one of the oldest and most vibrant spiritual texts in the world. He explains what yoga is, how it works, and exactly how to purify the mind and let it settle into absolute stillness. This stillness is our own Self. It is the indispensable ground for Enlightenment, which is the ultimate goal of all our aspirations.Alistair Shearer’s lucid introduction and superb translation, fully preserving Patanjali’s jewel-like style, bring these ancient but vital teachings to those who seek the path of self-knowledge today.Bell Tower’s series, Sacred Teachings, offers essential spiritual classics from all traditions. Still above is Vaikuntha, heaven, abode of God.The doorkeepers of Vaikuntha are the twins, Jaya and Vijaya, both whose names mean \'victory\'. One keeps you in Swarga; the other raises you into Vaikuntha.In Vaikuntha there is bliss forever, in Swarga there is pleasure for only as long as you deserve. What is the difference between Jaya and Vijaya? Solve this puzzle and you will solve the mystery of the Mahabharata.In this enthralling retelling of India\'s greatest epic, the Mahabharata originally known as Jaya, Devdutt Pattanaik seamlessly weaves into a single narrative plots from the Sanskrit classic as well as its many folk and regional variants, including the Pandavani of Chhattisgarh, Gondhal of Maharashtra, Terukkuttu of Tamil Nadu and Yakshagana of Karnataka.Richly illustrated with over 250 line drawings by the author, the 108 chapters abound with little-known details such as the names of the hundred Kauravas, the worship of Draupadi as a goddess in Tamil Nadu, the stories of Astika, Madhavi, Jaimini, Aravan and Barbareek, the Mahabharata version of the Shakuntalam and the Ramayana, and the dating of the war based on astronomical data.With clarity and simplicity, the tales in this elegant volume reveal the eternal relevance of the Mahabharata, the complex and disturbing meditation on the human condition that has <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10439951095212458995">Chakravarti Rajagopalachari, informally called Rajaji or C.R., was an Indian lawyer, independence activist, politician, writer and statesman. Rajagopalachari was the last Governor-General of India. He also served as leader of the Indian National Congress, Premier of the Madras Presidency, Governor of West Bengal, Minister for Home Affairs of the Indian Union and Chief Minister of Madras state and </span>
  <span id="freeText10439951095212458995" style="display:none">Chakravarti Rajagopalachari, informally called Rajaji or C.R., was an Indian lawyer, independence activist, politician, writer and statesman. Rajagopalachari was the last Governor-General of India. He also served as leader of the Indian National Congress, Premier of the Madras Presidency, Governor of West Bengal, Minister for Home Affairs of the Indian Union and Chief Minister of Madras state and as such, he rendered yeomen service to the nation.<br />Rajagopalachari founded the Swatantra Party and was one of the first recipients of India's highest civilian award, the Bharat Ratna. He vehemently opposed the use of nuclear weapons and was a proponent of world peace and disarmament. During his lifetime, he also acquired the nickname 'Mango of Salem'.<br />Rajaji was a great patriot, astute politician, incisive thinker, great visionary and one of the greatest statesmen of all time. He was a close associate of Mahatma Gandhi, hailed as <b> conscious-keeper </b> of the Mahatma.<br />Rajaji was closely associated with Kulapati Munshiji and he was among the distinguished founder-members of the Bhavan (Bharatiya Vidya Bhavan). The Bhavan has published 18 books by him so far, the copyright of which he gifted to the Bhavan.<br />Rajaji wrote not only in English but also in chaste Tamil, his mother-tongue. He was at his best as a short-story writer. <br />Rajaji passed away in 1972 at the age of 94.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10439951095212458995" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
